  • Phosphagen system#

    • $\mathrm{ADP} + \mathrm{Creatine Phosphate (CP)}\xleftrightarrow{\text{Creatine Kinase}}$\mathrm{ATP} + \mathrm{Creatine Phosphate (CP)}

    • AMP is a powerful stimulant of glycolysis

Ch.4 Endocrine Responses to Resistance Exercise#

understand basic concepts of endocrinology, including what hormones are and how they interact with each other and target tissues;â€¢ explain the physiological roles of anabolic hormones;â€¢ describe hormonal responses to resistance exercise; andâ€¢ develop training programs that demonstrate an understanding of human endocrine responses. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 81 hl-color:: blue

  • General Adaptation Syndrome: how the adrenal gland responds to a noxious stimulus (stressor)
    initial alarm reaction that includes a reduction of function -> increase in resistance to the stress above the previous baseline function. (adaptation)

  • The specific force produced in activated fibers stimulates receptor and membrane sensitivities to anabolic factors, including hormones, which lead to muscle growth and strength changes. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 88 hl-color:: yellow

Ch.5 Adaptations to Anaerobic Training Programs#

differentiate between aerobic training adaptations and the anatomical, physiological, and performance adaptations following anaerobic training;â€¢ discuss the central and peripheral neural adaptations to anaerobic training;â€¢ understand how manipulating the acute training variables of a periodized program can alter bone, muscle, and connective tissue;â€¢ explain the acute and chronic effects of anaerobic training on the endocrine system;â€¢ elucidate the acute and chronic effects of anaerobic training on the cardiovascular system;â€¢ recognize the causes, signs, symptoms, and effects of anaerobic overtraining and detraining; andâ€¢ discuss how anaerobic training programs have the potential to enhance muscular strength, muscular endurance, power, flexibility, and motor performance. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 103 hl-color:: blue

  • Anaerobic training â†’ â†‘ left ventricular wall thickness, not chamber size (which is aerobic).

  • Overview#

    • Anaerobic training: high-intensity, intermittent (e.g., resistance, plyometrics, sprinting)

    • èƒ½æºç³»çµ±ï¼šPhosphagenï¼ˆ<10sï¼‰èˆ‡Glycolyticï¼ˆ20â€“60sï¼‰ç³»çµ±ç‚ºä¸»

    • é©æ‡‰åŒ…æ‹¬ï¼šâ†‘ strength, power, hypertrophy, endurance, coordination, neural & hormonal changes

  • Neural Adaptations#

    • â†‘ neural drive: â†‘ agonist recruitment, firing rate, synchronization

    • â†“ inhibition (Golgi tendon organs)

    • Central adaptations: â†‘ motor cortex activity

    • Motor unit: â†‘ fast-twitch recruitment, selective recruitment under high load

      • size principle: é€šå¸¸å…ˆå¾µæ‹› type I, ä¸è¡Œæ‰å»æ‰¾ type II [:span] ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 107 hl-color:: yellow

      • selective recruitment è¨“ç·´å¾Œå¯å„ªå…ˆå¾µæ‹› high-threshold motor units

With heavy resistance training, all muscle fibers get larger (i.e., hypertrophy) because motor units are recruited in a sequential order by their size to produce high levels of force. In advanced lifters, the central nervous system may adapt by allowing well-trained athletes to recruit some motor units in a nonconsecutive order, by recruiting larger ones first to promote greater production of power or speed in a movement. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 107 hl-color:: yellow

+ **NMJ**: â†‘ area, ACh receptor density


  + NMJ was found to increase in its total area.

ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 108 hl-color:: red

+ **Reflex potentiation**: â†‘ stretch reflex response

+ EMG: the onset of hypertrophy is associated with a decline in EMG activity

ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 109 hl-color:: yellow

  + cross-education: åªç·´ä¸€é‚Š å¦å¤–ä¸€é‚Šstrength and neural activity ä¹Ÿæœƒè®Šå¼·

  + æ²’è¨“ç·´ group: bilateral deficit : The force produced when both limbs contract together is lower than the sum of the forces they produce when contracting unilaterally

  + > reduced antagonist cocontraction following resistance training, resulting in an increase in net force without an increase in agonist motor unit recruitment

  • Muscular Adaptations#

    • Hypertrophy: â†‘ CSA of Type I & II (esp. II), 6â€“10 wk typical onset

The process of hypertrophy involves both an increase in the synthesis of the contractile proteins actin and myosin within the myofibril and an increase in the number of myofibrils within the muscle fiber itself. The new myofilaments are added to the external layers of the myofibril, resulting in an increase in its diameter ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 110 hl-color:: yellow

+ **Fiber type shift**: IIx â†’ IIa (â†‘ fatigue resistance)

+ **Architecture**: â†‘ pennation angle

+ **Enzymes**: â†‘ creatine phosphokinase, myokinase, PFK

+ **Substrate**: â†‘ resting ATP, CP,

  • Connective Tissue Adaptations#

    • Bone: â†‘ BMD via minimal essential strain (MES = ~1/10 of fracture force)

      • Select multijoint, structural exercises that involve many muscle groups at once. Avoid isolated, single-joint movements. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 115 hl-color:: yellow

      • Select exercises that direct axial force vectors through the spine and hip and apply heavier loads than single-joint assistance exercises.â€¢ Use the principle of progressive overload to stress the musculoskeletal system, and continue to progressively increase load as the tissues become accustomed to the stimulus.â€¢ Use both heavy-load exercises and ballistic or high-impact exercises to expose the bone to different intensities of force.â€¢ By varying exercise selection, it is possible to change the distribution of force insults and present a unique stimulus for new bone formation ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 115 hl-color:: yellow

    • Tendon/Ligament: â†‘ collagen fibril packing, strength, diameter

      • tendon stiffness (force transmission per unit of strain, or tendon elongation) increases as a result of resistance training

      • progressive high-intensity loading patterns using external resistances

      • Forces should be exerted throughout the full range of motion of a joint, and wherever possible multiple-joint exercises should be used ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 117 hl-color:: yellow

    • Cartilage: â†‘ thickness via moderate loading (not excessive)

      • Moderate-intensity anaerobic exercise seems to be adequate ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 117 hl-color:: yellow

      • Strenuous exercise does not appear to cause any degenerative joint disease when progressively overloaded appropriately ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 117 hl-color:: yellow

  • Endocrine Adaptations#

    • Acute hormonal response: â†‘ testosterone, GH, IGF-1, cortisol

    • Chronic changes: resting hormones often unchanged

    • Receptor content: may â†“ transiently post-exercise (e.g., androgen receptor) but Upregulated within 48â€“72h

    • Training prescription: high volume + short rest â†’ â†‘ anabolic hormone spikes

  • Cardiovascular and Respiratory Adaptations#

    • Acute: â†‘ HR, SV, CO, BP

    • Chronic: â†‘ left ventricle wall thickness (not volume), improved heart strength

      • both systolic and diastolic blood pressure decreased by 2% to 4% as an adaptation to resistance training ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 120 hl-color:: red

      • rateâ€“pressure product (heart rate î€³ systolic blood pressure; a measure of myocardial work) has been shown to either remain constant or decrease following resistance training ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 120 hl-color:: yellow

    • Ventilatory response: modest â†‘, not as significant as in aerobic training

  • Compatibility of Aerobic and Anaerobic Modes of Training#

    • simultaneous sprint and aerobic endurance training decreased sprint speed and jump power ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 121 hl-color:: yellow

    • no adverse effects on aerobic power resulting from heavy resistance exercise ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 121 hl-color:: yellow

    • heavy resistance training has very limited, if any, negative effects on aerobic power (197) but instead can serve to actually enhance performance in endurance sports ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 121 hl-color:: yellow

  • Detraining#

    • Detraining: â†“ neural activation, CSA, Type II fiber size, ATP-CP stores

  • High Yield / Take-Home Points#

    • Neural adaptations precede hypertrophy

    • Type IIx â†’ IIa fiber transition enhances fatigue resistance

    • â†‘ buffering capacity from glycolytic stress = â†‘ muscular endurance

    • MES principle governs bone adaptations

    • Short rest + high volume â†’ greater hormonal spike (GH, testosterone)

    • Connective tissue adapts slower, needs progressive overload

    • Concurrent aerobic + anaerobic training may hinder power development

  • Self-Efficacy#

    • Self-confidence : belief that one can successfully perform a desired behavior Self-efficacy : situationally specific form of self-confidence the perception of oneâ€™s ability to perform a given task in a specific situation

    • better predictor of task execution than either arousal or anxiety

    • Bandura’s theory: é€™å¹¾é …çµ„æˆå› ç´ 

      • Performance accomplishmentsâ€”past experiences of success or failure

      • Vicarious experiencesâ€”watching others (modeling)

      • Verbal persuasionâ€”encouragement from self or others

      • Imaginal experienceâ€”using imagery to see oneself perform

      • Physiological statesâ€”perception of arousal as facilitative or debilitative

      • Emotional statesâ€”affect or mood

    • Self-efficacy influences peopleâ€™s choice of activity, their level of effort in that activity, and how much persistence they will have in the face of challenging obstacles. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 183 hl-color:: yellow

  • Goal Setting ğŸ¯#

**Pursuing progressively challenging tasks with defined performance criteria **

+ Goals direct an athleteâ€™s attention by prioritizing efforts.

Goals increase effort because of the contingency of success on goal attainment. Goals increase positive reinforcement through the feedback given to athletes.

+ ç¨®é¡ï¼š

  + **Process**ï¼ˆæŠ€è¡“åŸ·è¡Œï¼Œå¦‚æ“ºè‡‚è§’åº¦ï¼‰

  + **Short-Term Goals** directly related to current training or competition and are guidelines that can be attained in a relatively short time frame

  + **Long-term goals** are those that overarch the series of linked short-term goals.

+ #### Feedback æ™‚æ©Ÿèˆ‡é »ç‡ â±ï¸

Feedback can be used to facilitate both learning and performance. The timing and frequency of the feedback have different influences on performance and learning. While concurrent feedback is beneficial for competition, delayed feedback that is initially frequent and decreases with time will facilitate learning of complex movement patterns. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 188 hl-color:: yellow

list pre-, during- and postcompetition nutrition recommendations for different sports;â€¢ provide guidelines for weight gain and weight loss;â€¢ recognize signs and symptoms of eating disorders;â€¢ understand the importance of having an intervention and referral system in place for athletes suspected of having an eating disorder;â€¢ recognize the prevalence and etiologies of obesity; andâ€¢ assist in the assessment process for obese individuals ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 217 hl-color:: blue

+ ### Carbohydrate Loading


  + commonly used carbohydrate loading regimen includes three days of a high-carbohydrate diet in concert with tapering exercise the week before competition and complete rest the day before the event.

provide reliable and up-to-date information to athletes on the risks and benefits of performance-enhancing substances, including anabolic steroids;â€¢ understand the efficacy and adverse effects of over-the-counter dietary supplements marketed to athletes for enhancing sport and exercise performance;â€¢ determine which performance-enhancing supplements are beneficial for strength/ power performance, endurance performance, or both; andâ€¢ distinguish between those performanceenhancing supplements that mimic the effects of hormones in the body and those that improve performance through some other means ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 241 hl-color:: blue

+ ### ğŸ”¹ b-Hydroxy-b-methylbutyrate (HMB)

derivative of the EAA leucine and its metabolite a-ketoisocaproic acid. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 255 hl-color:: yellow

å¢åŠ è›‹ç™½è³ªåˆæˆ æ¸›å°‘åˆ†è§£ by ubiquitin. åˆæ³•.

((684cf602-7ade-4379-8c23-94124d8d7209))

  + Wilson GJ, Wilson JM, Manninen AH. Effects of beta-hydroxy-beta-methylbutyrate (HMB) on exercise performance and body composition across varying levels of age, sex, and training experience: A review. Nutr Metab (Lond). 2008 Jan 3;5:1. doi: 10.1186/1743-7075-5-1. PMID: 18173841; PMCID: PMC2245953.

While various studies analyzed in this manuscript support the efficacy of HMB as an effective ergogenic aid for athletes that decreases DOMS, markers of muscle damage, and body fat, while increasing various markers of performance, including LBM and strength in resistance trained athletes, and OBLA and VO2 peak in endurance trained athletes, a number of studies analyzed did not support the efficacy of HMB supplementation.

There is compelling evidence that HMB supplementation may be useful for clinical muscle wasting conditions including AIDS, cancer, bed-rest, and during periods of caloric deficits. HMB also appears to be safe, and may improve various markers of health, including blood pressure and LDL-cholesterol. When supplementing with HMB, current evidence suggests that 1 g of HMB should be consumed 3 times per day, for a total of 3 g of HMB daily (or 38 mg/kg of bodyweight). However, more studies are needed to determine the optimal dosage and frequency of HMB supplementation, and the overall efficacy of HMB supplementation as an ergogenic aid for athletes.

  + MBC èˆ‡ä»¥ä¸‹æœ‰æ­£ç›¸é—œï¼šrepeated sprint ability, anaerobic threshold, training volume, high-intensity capacityï¼ˆe.g. soccer, sprint, hockey, cycling, rowing, triathlonï¼‰

  + The ability to regulate H + concentration in skeletal muscle during high-intensity exercise has been termed muscle buffering capacity (MBC)

      • | é¡å‹ | å®šç¾© | é—œéµå·®ç•° | ç¯„ä¾‹ | | —- | —- | —- | | Concurrent Validity | æ¸¬é©—çµæœæ˜¯å¦èˆ‡ç›®å‰å¯æ¥å—çš„é‡‘æ¨™æº–æ¸¬é©—ä¸€è‡´ | âœ… åŒæ™‚æ¯”å° | å‚ç›´è·³ vs. é«˜ç§‘æŠ€åŠ›æ¿åŒæ­¥æ¸¬çˆ†ç™¼åŠ› | | Predictive Validity | æ¸¬é©—çµæœæ˜¯å¦èƒ½é æ¸¬æœªä¾†è¡¨ç¾ | âœ… æœªä¾†å°å‘ | 40-yard dash æ¸¬æˆç¸¾æ˜¯å¦èƒ½é æ¸¬ WR è¡¨ç¾ | | Discriminant Validity | æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦å€åˆ†ä¸åŒèƒ½åŠ›æˆ–æ—ç¾¤ | âœ… èƒ½å€åˆ†æ‰æœ‰æ„ç¾© | ä¸€é …è‚ŒåŠ›æ¸¬é©—ä¸æ‡‰èˆ‡æŸ”è»Ÿåº¦é«˜åº¦ç›¸é—œ |

      • Concurrent validity is the extent to which test scores are associated with those of other accepted tests that measure the same ability

        • Convergent validity is evidenced by high positive correlation between results of the test being assessed and those of the recognized measure of the construct(the â€œgold standardâ€).

        • Convergent Validity æ¸¬é©—èˆ‡ç†è«–ä¸Šç›¸ä¼¼çš„å…¶ä»–æ¸¬é©—çµæœä¸€è‡´æ€§ é€šå¸¸é€é**ç›¸é—œä¿‚æ•¸ï¼ˆrï¼‰**æª¢é©—ï¼Œr > 0.7 é€šå¸¸ç‚ºä½³ çœ‹æ˜¯å¦â€œèšåˆâ€åˆ°åŒæ§‹å¿µ

      • Predictive validity corresponds with future behavior or performance.

      • Discriminant validity distinguish between two different constructs and is evidenced by a low correlation between the results of the test and those of tests of a different construct

      • Concurrent = ç¾åœ¨ vs. æ¨™æº– â†’ ã€Œæˆ‘é€™å€‹æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦è·Ÿå…¬èªæº–çš„å°ä¸Šï¼Ÿã€

      • Predictive = ç¾åœ¨ vs. æœªä¾† â†’ ã€Œæˆ‘é€™æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦é æ¸¬èª°èƒ½æˆåŠŸï¼Ÿã€

+ ### Aerobic Endurance Testing in the Heat


  + **Using temperature limits at least 5Â°F (3 Â°C) below those listed is recommended, especially on sunny days, to provide a safety margin and enable better test performance.**

  + acclimate at least a week from cold climate

  • Test Administrationï¼ˆæ¸¬é©—å®‰æ’ï¼‰#

+ ### Test sequence


  + 1. Nonfatiguing tests (e.g., height, weight, flexibility, skinfold and girth measurements, vertical jump)

  + **2. Agility tests (e.g., T-test, pro agility test)**

  + 3. Maximum power and strength tests (e.g., 1RM power clean, 1RM squat)

  + 4. Sprint tests (e.g., 40 m sprint with split times at10 m and 20 m)

  + 5. Local muscular endurance tests (e.g., push-up test)

  + 6. Fatiguing anaerobic capacity tests (e.g., 300-yard[275 m] shuttle)

  + 7. Aerobic capacity tests (e.g., 1.5-mile [2.4 km] run or Yo-Yo intermittent recovery test)

  • Strength#

    • 1RM Bench Press#

type:: test category:: strength tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Olympic barbell, flat bench, weight plates, collars, spotter arms or racks

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1â€“2 trained spotters, 1 test administrator

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. General warm-up + specific warm-upï¼ˆ5â€“10 reps with light loadï¼‰

  2. Rest 1 min â†’ 3â€“5 reps @ ~60â€“70% est. 1RM

  3. Rest 2 min â†’ 2â€“3 reps @ ~80% 1RM

  4. Rest 2â€“4 min â†’ attempt 1RM

  5. Increase weight 5â€“10% per attempt until failure

  6. Full ROM: bar touches chestï¼ˆno bounceï¼‰, full elbow lockout

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:

      • Male collegiate average: ~1.05Ã—BWï¼ˆgoodï¼‰ï¼›Elite >1.5Ã—BW

      • Combine settings often use submax RMï¼ˆe.g. 225lb repsï¼‰ instead

    • 13.2 1RM Bench Pull#

type:: test alias:: category:: strength tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Flat bench with support for horizontal pull, barbell, lifting straps optional

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to confirm rep standard

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Lie prone on bench, barbell racked under chest level

  2. Pull bar upward to touch underside of bench without torso motion

  3. Lower under control, full extension

  4. Use same warm-up & 1RM progression as bench press

    • âš ï¸ Caveats:

      • Torso must stay in contact with bench at all times é ­ä¹Ÿæ˜¯ Overextension = invalid rep Should not allow excessive elbow flare

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:

      • Often paired with 1RM Bench Press to assess push-pull ratio Ideal Push:Pull ratio â‰ˆ 1:1 in balanced athletes

    • 13.3 1RM Back Squat#

type:: test category:: strength tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Olympic barbell, squat rack or power cage, safety pins

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 2 trained spotters, 1 ç´€éŒ„

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Warm-up progression same as Bench Press

  2. Bar rests on upper trapsï¼ˆnot neckï¼‰

  3. Feet shoulder-width, toes slightly out

  4. Descent: thighs parallel to floor = minimum depth

  5. Ascend to full extension without bar shifting

    • âš ï¸ Caveats:

      • Monitor for valgus collapse, trunk lean, and heel lift Full depth standard = thigh parallel Spotters must coordinate hand placement & timing

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:

      • Male collegiate average: 1.5â€“2.0Ã—BWï¼ˆelite >2.5Ã—BWï¼‰ Excellent for force development assessment Pair with VJ to evaluate strengthâ€“power profile

+ #### 13.5 Standing Long Jump

type:: test category:: power tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Measuring tape (cm/in), flat jumping area at least 20 feet (6 m) in length

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: One distance judge, one recorder

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Feet shoulder-width behind start line

  2. Arm swing + 2-foot takeoff
    3. Land with both feet, no backward fall

  3. Measure from start line to rear-most heel contact

  4. Best of 3 trials used to 1cm

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:
      Male college athletes average: ~2.3â€“2.5m
      High correlation with sprint acceleration and leg power Elite PR50 250 280 PR90 315 375 [:span] ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 316 hl-color:: yellow

    • 13.6 Vertical Jump#

type:: test alias:: VJ, CMJ, Countermovement Jump category:: power tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: chalk, wall, measuring tape or Vertec

ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 284 hl-color:: yellow

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 tester/recorder

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

è·é›¢ 15cm from wall

  1. Measure standing reach (1-hand flat reach)

  2. Perform countermovement (quick dip) + jump and tap highest marker

  3. Record difference between jump height and standing reach

  4. 3 attempts, best score recorded

    • âš ï¸ Caveats:
      No running or step-in allowed
      Fingertip must clearly tap marker
      Must land with control to count

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:
      Male college athlete norm: 50. women: 40-50 Can be used to calculate Peak Power via Sayers formula

    • 13.7 Static Vertical Jump#

type:: test alias:: Squat Jump, SJ category:: power tags::

  + ğŸ” Procedure:  same as VJ but no counter-movement

  1. Begin in squat position (~90Â° knee angle), no countermovement

  2. Hold 2â€“3 sec, then jump vertically as high as possible

  3. Hands on hips (if required)

  4. Best of 3 jumps recorded

    • âš ï¸ Caveats:
      No arm swing or countermovement permitted
      Failure to hold preload squat = invalid rep
      High variation in beginner execution

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:
      Isolates concentric power (no stretch-shortening cycle)
      Useful in profiling SSC utilization compared to CMJ

The ratio of the vertical jump height with countermovement to squat jump height can be calculated as the eccentric utilization ratio ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 286 hl-color:: yellow

+ #### 13.8 Reactive Strength Index

type:: test alias:: RSI category:: power / plyometric tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Contact mat or dual force plates + timer , boxes

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1  with stopwatch or sensor interface

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

ç®±å­åœ°æ¯¯è·é›¢è‡³å°‘20cm

  1. Perform depth (drop) jump from 20â€“60 cm box . hands on hips.

  2. Upon ground contact, immediately rebound vertically

  3. Measure flight time and contact time

  4. RSI = jump height Ã· contact time

    • âš ï¸ Caveats:
      Delayed rebound or pause = invalid
      Ensure consistent box height across testing
      Monitor contact mat sensitivity

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:
      Higher RSI = better stretch-shortening cycle efficiency
      Elite athletes RSI ~2.0+ (msâ»Â¹)

    • 13.9 Margaria-Kalamen Test#

type:: test alias:: Stair Sprint Power Test category:: power tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Stopwatch,  >9-step stair with ~17.8 cm steps, åŠ©è·‘6m

[:span] ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 288 hl-color:: yellow

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 timer

ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Start 6m from base of stairs

  2. Sprint and step on 3rd, 6th, and 9th steps as fast as possible

  3. Time measured between 3rd and 9th step contact

  4. Power = (body mass (newton) Ã— vertical distance) Ã· time

    • âš ï¸ Caveats:
      Only valid if correct steps are hit cleanly
      Shoes must fully contact step
      Risk of trip if timing is off

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes:
      Assesses short burst anaerobic power
      Better for football/track athletes than general populations watts 20-30 exellent: 2000, 1600 å¥³ç”Ÿexellent=ç”·ç”Ÿaverage 30-40 exellent: 1600, 1200

  • Anaerobic#

    • 13.10 300-Yard Shuttle#

type:: test alias:: Anaerobic Capacity Shuttle category:: anaerobic capacity tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Stopwatch, 25-yard marked lanes, cones

  + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Place 2 cones 25 yards apart

  2. Sprint 25 yards out and back = 1 rep

  3. Complete 6 total reps (6 Ã— 50y = 300 yards)

  4. Time entire effort with stopwatch

  5. Perform 2 trials separated by full recoveryï¼ˆ~5 minsï¼‰

  6. Average of both trials is score

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Turns must be sharp, touching or crossing line Test must be conducted on flat, non-slip surface Fatigue may cause pacingâ€”encourage maximal effort

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Assesses anaerobic lactic capacity + repeated sprint fatigue Often used for football, soccer, basketball athletes Collegiate male average: 55â€“60 sec. Normal: 72

  • Local Muscular Endurance#

    • 13.11 Partial Curl-Up#

type:: test alias:: Abdominal Endurance Test category:: local muscular endurance tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Mat, masking tape (**7.6 cm** / 4.5 in spacing), metronome

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 rep counter
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Lie supine, knees at 90Â°, arms by sides, fingers to tape

  2. Metronome at 40 bpm controls cadence

  3. Curl up until fingertips reach second tape, then return

  4. Max reps = 75 or until failure

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Movement must be pacedâ€”no rushing or skipping beats Pelvis/feet must remain on floor Ensure smooth motion; no jerking

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Targets rectus abdominis + obliques endurance Less stressful than full sit-up for lumbar spine Valid for general population, not max effort athletes exellent: 75 55 average: 30-40 ; 20-30

    • 13.12 Push-Up Test#

type:: test alias:: Upper-Body Endurance Test category:: local muscular endurance tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Mat or flat surface

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 rep counter
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Male: standard push-up position
    Female: modified (knees on ground)

  3. As many reps as possible without time limit

  4. Test ends when failure or break in form

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Military and fire academies often use for screening Exellent: Male 30â€“40; Female 25â€“30 Not highly correlated with absolute strength, more with endurance

    • 13.13 YMCA Bench Press Test#

type:: test category:: local muscular endurance tags::

    • 13.14 1.5-Mile (2.4 km) Run#

type:: test category:: aerobic capacity tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Measured 1.5-mile (2.4 km) flat course, stopwatch

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 safety observer if needed
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Subject runs 1.5 miles as fast as possible

  2. Time is recorded in minutes:seconds

  3. Optional: use formula to estimate VOâ‚‚max
    VOâ‚‚max = (483 / time in minutes) + 3.5

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Heat, wind, elevation affect performance Medical clearance may be needed for high-risk groups Warm-up recommended; pacing strategies vary

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Best for trained runners and conditioned individuals College male norm: ~10:30â€“12:00; female ~12:00â€“14:00 High face validity for distance/endurance sports PR50 : VO2max 45 12:00ã€‚ 38 15:00 PR90: VO2max 54 9:34ã€‚ 47 11:00

    • 13.15 12-Minute Run (Cooper Test)#

type:: test alias:: Cooper Test category:: aerobic capacity tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Track or treadmill, cones for markers, stopwatch

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 counter/observer
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Subject runs for 12 continuous minutes

  2. Total distance covered (meters) is recorded

  3. VOâ‚‚max estimation (meters):
    VOâ‚‚max = (Distance in meters âˆ’ 504.9) Ã· 44.73

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Pacing is critical; early overexertion leads to drop-off Not ideal for untrained or clinical populations Must measure distance precisely

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Cooper test used globally for endurance assessments Used in military, soccer, and fire academies Male average: 2.4 ; Female: 2.1 km PR90 2.8ã€‚ 2.5ã€‚

    • 13.16 Yo-Yo Intermittent Recovery Test (Level 1)#

type:: test alias:: Yo-Yo IRT1 category:: aerobic capacity + intermittent recovery tags:: [:span] ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 294 hl-color:: yellow

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Cones, 20m lane, audio CD/app, measuring tape

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 observer
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Sprint 20m â†’ turn â†’ return to start within audio cue

  2. 5â€“10 sec recovery in 5m jog zone

  3. Continue until failure (2 misses = termination)

  4. Record last completed stage and distance

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Requires space, clear audio system, and athlete familiarity Motivation & familiarity affect result consistency Not valid if cone touches or cut corners

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Better reflects team sport conditioning than steady-state tests High correlation with repeat sprint and VOâ‚‚max ğŸ§ Elite male: >2000m; average trained: 1400â€“1800m

    • 13.17 Maximal Aerobic Speed (MAS) Test#

type:: test alias:: VÌ‡Oâ‚‚max Speed Test category:: aerobic capacity tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: 200m+ track, cones, audio signal or pacing system

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 timekeeper
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Athlete runs at increasing speeds, guided by audio pacing

  2. Speed increases ~1 km/h every 2 minutes

  3. Continue until failure to keep pace

  4. MAS = highest speed maintained for full stage

    • V.O2 max = 3.5 x MAS (km/hr)

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Needs space and clear markers for distance Best administered outdoors with clear pacing Environmental factors affect max speed

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: MAS used to prescribe aerobic intervals (e.g., 120% MAS) More precise than Cooper/Yo-Yo for programming intensity Elite endurance MAS ~18â€“20 km/h

  • Agility#

    • 13.18 T-Test#

type:: test alias:: T-Agility Drill category:: agility tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: 4 cones (5-10-5 yd), stopwatch, tape measure

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Set cones in â€œTâ€ formationï¼š10 yd top, 5 yd stem

  2. Sprint forward 10 yd, shuffle left 5 yd, shuffle right 10 yd, shuffle left 5 yd, backpedal 10 yd

  3. Must touch base of each cone with hand

  4. Time recorded from start to full return

    • best time of two trials

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Hand must touch cone base or trial invalid Footwork form affects time significantly Lateral shuffles = no crossover steps

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Tests lateral + linear movement transitions Male avg (collegiate): ~9.5â€“10.5 sec Common in football, basketball, and volleyball

    • 13.19 Hexagon Test#

type:: test alias:: Hex Jump Test category:: agility + coordination tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Athletic tape to mark hexagon (24 in sides), stopwatch

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 floor judge
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Form hexagon with 6 equal 24-in sides, 120Â° angles

  2. Jump over each side and back to center in clockwise direction

  3. 3 full revolutions (~18 jumps), time recorded

  4. Repeat in opposite direction (optional)

    • best time of three trials is recorded

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Only 2-foot jumps allowed Misstep = restart or penalty Requires spatial awareness and coordination

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Tests multidirectional reactivity and lower limb stability Norm: Male ~12â€“13 sec; Female ~13â€“15 sec

    • 13.20-pro-agility-test#

type:: test alias:: 20 yard Shuttle, 5-10-5 Shuttle category:: agility tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: 3 cones spaced 5 yards apart, stopwatch or timing gates

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Start straddling center cone line (three-point stance)

  2. Sprint 5 yd right, then 10 yd left, then return 5 yd to center

  3. Must touch base of each outer cone with hand

  4. Best of 2 trials recorded

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Must touch line accurately for valid rep Change-of-direction footwork affects time greatly Lateral lean control is key

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Gold-standard agility test for football & rugby Male norm: ~4.5â€“4.8 sec (elite <4.5 sec) Great for assessing deceleration & re-acceleration

    • 13.21 505 Agility Test#

type:: test alias:: Single-Direction Change of Direction category:: agility tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Cones, 15m flat running surface, stopwatch or timing gates

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Set cones at start, 5m, and 15m marks

  2. Athlete sprints forward from 15m mark

  3. Time is recorded over final 10m segment (from 10m â†’ 15m and back to 10m)

  4. At 15m cone, athlete cuts 180Â°, sprints back 5m

  5. Test best of two trials per leg

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Start is moving; donâ€™t time first 10m (acceleration phase) Use both left and right leg cuts for asymmetry screening Clear cone contact and change of direction technique critical

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Common in rugby, soccer, court sports Elite athletes: ~2.2â€“2.4 sec Measures deceleration + re-acceleration ability in one direction

    • 13.22 Straight-Line Sprint Test#

type:: test alias:: 40 Yard Sprint category:: speed tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Measured distance (10m/20m/40yd), timing gates or stopwatch

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1â€“2 timers or automatic timing system
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Warm up fully; perform practice starts

  2. Sprint through measured line (10m, 20m, 40yd, etc.)
    at least 2 min rest

  3. Best of 2 trials

  4. Use split times if available to assess acceleration profile

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Athlete must start from same stance each trial Avoid leaning or diving early into the line Timing accuracy keyâ€”gates better than stopwatch

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: 10m = accelerationï¼›40yd = football standard Elite 10m: <1.8 secï¼›40m: <5.3 Use multiple splits for sprint profiling

  • Balance and Stability#

    • 13.23 Balance Error Scoring System (BESS)#

type:: test alias:: Static Postural Stability Test category:: balance and stability tags:: [:span] ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 300 hl-color:: yellow

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Foam pad, stopwatch, clipboard

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to count errors

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

    + 1. 3 stances Ã— 2 surfaces (firm + foam)ï¼š

a) Double-leg stance
b) Single-leg stance (non-dominant foot)
c) Tandem (heel-to-toe) stance (dominant foot in front of the nondominant foot)

    + 2. Each held for 20 sec, eyes closed, hands on hips

    + 3. Count errors (max 10 per condition):

opening eyes; lifting hands from hips; touchdown of nonstance foot; step, hop, or other lifting forefeet or heel moving hip into more than 30Â° of hip flexion or abduction out of position for more than 5 seconds

    + 4. Total errors across all 6 conditions = score

  + âš ï¸ Caveats:

Very sensitive to fatigue, concussion, inner ear issues Inconsistent flooring or ambient noise can affect results

  + ğŸ“Œ Notes:

Widely used in concussion screening Lower total errors = better balance Norms: Healthy athletes = ~10â€“15 total errors

+ #### 13.24 Star Excursion Balance Test (SEBT)

type:: test alias:: Dynamic Balance Reach Test category:: balance and stability tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Tape or grid to form 8-star directions (120Â° separation)

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to mark distances

    + ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Athlete stands on one leg in center

  2. Reaches opposite foot in 3 main directions (anterior, posteromedial, posterolateral)

  3. Reach as far as possible while maintaining single-leg stance rest at least 15 secs

  4. Distance recorded per direction; 3 trials each (take average)

  5. Normalize score to leg lengthï¼ˆreach Ã· limb length Ã— 100ï¼‰

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Loss of balance, foot tap, or shift = invalid trial Leg length must be accurately measured (ASIS â†’ medial malleolus) Allow practice trials for familiarization

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Effective for injury risk screeningï¼ˆesp. ankle/kneeï¼‰ Composite score <94% = â†‘ injury risk Dynamic alternative to BESS

  • Flexibility#

    • 13.25 Sit-and-Reach Test#

type:: test category:: flexibility tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Sit-and-reach box with ruler or yardstick

24 inches(61 cm) long across the measuring stick and at a right angle to it at the 15-inch (38 cm) mark ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to monitor technique and record score
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Athlete sits, legs extended, feet flat against box, shoes off - feet 12 inches (30 cm) apart

  2. Hands overlapped, arms extended forward

  3. Reach forward slowly and hold for 2 sec

  4. Record best of 3 trials

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Jerking motion or bouncing invalidates trial Does not isolate hamstrings (also influenced by spine, glutes) Norms vary based on age/gender

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: One of the most common general flexibility tests Useful for group fitness screening Not recommended for diagnosing mobility restrictions

    • 13.26 Overhead Squat Test#

type:: test
category:: flexibility / movement screen
tags::

+ ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Wooden dowel or PVC pipe, open space or grid mat

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to score posture deviations
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Dowel held overhead (elbows locked), feet shoulder-width
    grip twice shoulder-width

  2. Athlete performs deep squat: hips below knee line

  3. View from front and side for compensation

  4. Grade based on quality of: torso angle, knee tracking, depth, arm position

    • âš ï¸ Caveats: Not standardized for scoring (subjective) Dowel mobility limits may mask true lower-body restrictions Heels may elevate with limited ankle dorsiflexion

    • ğŸ“Œ Notes: Used in FMS (Functional Movement Screen) protocols Good for identifying mobility + motor control deficits Often combined with follow-up movement correctives

  • Body Comp#

    • 13.27 Skinfold Measurements#

type:: test alias:: Subcutaneous Body Fat Estimation category:: body composition tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Skinfold calipers, marker, measurement guide

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 skilled technician (training required)

  + ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Identify measurement sites (Jackson-Pollock 3- or 7-site protocols)
    e.g., chest, abdomen, thigh (men) / triceps, suprailiac, thigh (women)

  2. Pinch skinfold perpendicular to site axis

  3. Measure to nearest 0.5 mm

  4. Perform 2 measurements/site (3 if variation >2 mm)

  5. Use formula to estimate % body fat

     + Chestâ€”a diagonal fold one-half the distance between the anterior axillary line and the nipple for men (figure 13.19a

ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 304 hl-color:: yellow

    + Abdomenâ€”a vertical fold 1 inch (2.5 cm) to the right (relative to the athlete) of the umbilicus (figure 13.19c)

ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 304 hl-color:: yellow

    + [:span]

ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 305 hl-color:: yellow

  + âš ï¸ Caveats:

Hydration, stress, and caliper pressure affect readings Must be done on right side of body Technique-sensitive; inter-rater error common

  + ğŸ“Œ Notes:

Fast, inexpensive field estimate of body fat Â±3.5% margin of error compared to DEXA Useful for longitudinal tracking

  • Anthropometry#

    • 13.28 Girth Measurements#

type:: test category:: anthropometry tags::

  + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Non-stretchable measuring tape

ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 trained technician, preferably same person over time
ğŸ” Procedure:

  1. Measure girth at standard anatomical landmarks: all right
    chest, arm, waist, hips, thigh, calf
    [:span] ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 306 hl-color:: yellow

  2. Ensure tape is horizontal, snug but not compressing skin

  3. Record to nearest 0.1 cm

  4. Use in body composition or performance profile analysis

âš ï¸ Caveats: Time of day, hydration, posture affect values Must maintain consistent anatomical landmarks Best used for trend tracking, not exact composition

  + ğŸ“Œ Notes:

Often used in police/fire/military screening Valuable for hypertrophy or atrophy monitoring More reliable than weight alone for body change assessment

+ ### Freq, Duration:


  + Stretching twice per week, for a minimum of five weeks, has been shown to significantly improve flexibility

  + All stretching sessions should be preceded by a period of general activity to raise muscle temperature.

  + monitored for a loss of sensation or radiating pain

  • Core Stability and Balance Training Methods#

    • Core: axial skeleton and all the soft tissues with proximal attachments that originate on the axial skeleton ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 427 hl-color:: yellow

      • æ ¸å¿ƒç©©å®š = å‹•ä½œæ•ˆç‡ + åŠ›é‡è½‰ç§»å¹³å°

    • Isolation exercises : dynamic or isometric muscle actions designed to isolate specific core musculature without the contribution of the lower and upper extremities

      • ex: plank, side plank

      • good for untrained and rehab. BUT not effective at improving sport performance

  • Variable Resistance Training Methodsï¼ˆå¯è®Šé˜»åŠ›è¨“ç·´æ³•ï¼‰#

é¡åˆ¥è£ç½®ç‰¹æ€§
ğŸª éŠæ¢ï¼ˆChainsï¼‰free-hanging steel chainsresistance â†‘ éš¨ barbell ä¸Šå‡
ğŸ§¶ å½ˆåŠ›å¸¶ï¼ˆBandsï¼‰looped elastic bandså½ˆæ€§ â†‘ å¼µåŠ›ï¼Œé©åˆåŠ é€Ÿå€æ®µè² è·
+ Constant external resistance


  + traditional resistance training methods, represents real life activities

+ Accommodating resistance (semi-isokinetic)


  + speed of movement to be controlled throughout entire ROM

  + Poor external validity, unlikely to provide adequate training stimulus

+ Variable resistance


  + Combats the changing mechanical advantage

maximize force application throughout the entire ROM

  + backsquat w/ bands - Take the other ends of the bands and place them over the ends of the bar so they rest against the outside of the weight plates on either side

    • Strongman Training#

      • Tire flipping

        • tire ä¸è©²æ¯” athlete é«˜

        • Three basic techniques:

          • Sumo: When tire is raised to hip or chest height, hands are rotated to allow forward pressing action with the arms

          • Back lift: deadlift stance ended with a forward-pressing motion

          • Shoulders against the tire: Preferred technique

      • Log Lifting

        • é¡ä¼¼åš clean , midrange grip to accommodate a pronated grip position

        • Can use %1RM from other lifts but may be more difficult to lift log

      • farmerâ€™s walk develops total body anaerobic endurance

      • kettlebells are probably best used as general preparation exercise

        • æ²’æœ‰å‚³çµ±æ–¹æ³•æœ‰æ•ˆ

Ch. 17 Program Design for Resistance Training#

evaluate the requirements and characteristics of a sport and assess an athlete for the purpose of designing a resistance training program;â€¢ select exercises based on type, sport specificity, technique experience, equipment availability, and time availability;â€¢ determine training frequency based on training status, sport season, load, exercise type, and other concurrent exercise;â€¢ arrange exercises in a training session according to their type;â€¢ determine 1-repetition maximum (1RM), predicted 1RM from a multiple RM, and RM loads;â€¢ assign load and repetitions based on the training goal;â€¢ know when and by how much an exercise load should be increased;â€¢ assign training volumes according to the athleteâ€™s training status and the training goal; andâ€¢ determine rest period lengths based on the training goal. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 455 hl-color:: blue

  • Undulating Versus linear Periodization Models#

    • daily undulating periodization model: large daily (i.e., within the microcycle) fluctuations in the load and volume (e.g., repetitions and volume-load) of assigned core resistance training exercises.

      • å¯èƒ½åœ¨ä¸€é€±å…§å°±æœ‰ hypertrophy, strength, power

    • æœ‰äº›äººè¦ºå¾—å¥½( â†“ neural fatigue) æœ‰äº›äººåå° (è¼ƒé«˜ volume, å®¹æ˜“ fatigue, å—å‚·)

  • Example

    • preseason: 3.5M, 3x/week RT - strength & power, plyometrics, anaerobic conditioning

    • In- season: 20 weeks. Goal: maintain strength, power, flexibility, anaerobic conditioning

      • 30mins, 1-3x/week, undulating regimen. Power + basketball specific core.

      • RT and Plyo alternate. Plyo 1-2x/week If no RT: 15-20mins short sprint.

      • +- speed, agility, flexibility training. è¨“ç·´ç›¡é‡é–“éš” 2-3å¤©ä¼‘æ¯

      • è‹¥é€²å…¥ conference & national tournamentï¼Œå¯é‡å•Ÿ mesocycle 2â€“3 èª¿æ•´å¼·åº¦ If æ¯”è³½æ»¿æª” focus on power & core exercise. game å‰ä¸€å¤© plyo or sprint.

    • Post : chill, fun, recreational game

    • Off : 14weeks, test 1st wk. (shoulder&hip mobility, 12 min run, 300 yard shuttle, line drill, T-test, vertical jump, skinfold measurement)

      • RT:

        • åˆæœŸï¼šå…¨èº«è¨“ç·´ï¼Œæ¯é€± 3 æ¬¡

  • Program design#

    • Resistance Training:

      • Delorme and Oxford method:

        • 3*10 with pyramid design Delorme - 3x10 @ 50% 10RM, 75% 10RM, and 100% 10RM respectively è¼•åˆ°é‡ Oxford - 3x10 @ 100% 10RM, 75% 10RM, 50% 10RM é‡åˆ°è¼•

daily adjustable progressive resistive exercise (DAPRE)#

    + 4*10 @ 50% 1RM, 6 @ 75% 1RM, max reps @ 100% estimated 1RM

+ Sport-specific movements and training structure

  + Ex: higher reps when rehabbing endurance athletes, lower for strength/power athletes

  • Reducing Risk of Injury and Reinjury#

    • Previous injury is one of the most substantial risk factors for additional injury

    • Risk factors for upper extremity injury : â†“ shoulder ROM, scapular dyskinesis, and â†“ shoulder strength

      • include ROM exercises and possibly the â€œThrowerâ€™s Tenâ€

    • Risk factors for lower extremity injury - â†“ balance, neuromuscular control during jump landing, and lower extremity strength

      • single leg squat, plyometric landings

weight plate: 18 inches wide

  • Arranging equipment#

    • Barbells and dumbbells should have a minimum of36 inches (91 cm) of space between other barbells and dumbbells to allow for movement between racks without danger to the lifter or spotter(s). ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 645 hl-color:: yellow

    • walkways at least 36in wide

    • at least 49 square feet (4.6 m2) of open space so that athletes can perform a dynamic warm-up ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 645 hl-color:: yellow

    • Circuit training

      • circuit training machines should be at least 24 inches (61 cm)â€”preferably 36 inches (91 cm)â€”away from each other in order to provide a wide enough walk space and some extra space for safety called a safety cushion. ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 645 hl-color:: yellow

      • Any designated walkways in the circuit training area should be between 4 and 7 feet (1.2 and 2.1 m) wide ls-type:: annotation hl-page:: 645 hl-color:: yellow

    • Racks and platforms should be spaced 3 to 4 feet (0.9 to 1.2 m) apart.

      • weightlifting racks should be bolted to the floor
